   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Social Progress Index</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:2018_Social_Progress_Index.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/2018_Social_Progress_Index.jpg/400px-2018_Social_Progress_Index.jpg" decoding="async" width="400" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/2018_Social_Progress_Index.jpg/600px-2018_Social_Progress_Index.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/2018_Social_Progress_Index.jpg/800px-2018_Social_Progress_Index.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1644" data-file-height="794"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:2018_Social_Progress_Index.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>2020 Social Progress Index
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <i><b>Social Progress Index</b></i> (<i><b>SPI</b></i>) measures the extent to which countries provide for the social and environmental needs of their citizens. Fifty-four indicators in the areas of basic human needs, foundations of well-being, and opportunity to progress show the relative performance of nations. The index is published by the nonprofit <a href="/wiki/Social_Progress_Imperative" title="Social Progress Imperative">Social Progress Imperative</a>, and is based on the writings of <a href="/wiki/Amartya_Sen" title="Amartya Sen">Amartya Sen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Douglass_North" title="Douglass North">Douglass North</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stiglitz" title="Joseph Stiglitz">Joseph Stiglitz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The SPI measures the well-being of a society by observing social and environmental outcomes directly rather than the economic factors. The social and environmental factors include wellness (including health, shelter and sanitation), equality, inclusion, sustainability and personal freedom and safety.<sup id="cite_ref-own-website_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-own-website-2">[2]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading">
       <input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none">
       <div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr">
        <h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span>
       </div> 
       <ul> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Introduction_and_methodology"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Introduction and methodology</span></a></li>

        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#2020_Rankings_and_scores_by_country"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">2020 Rankings and scores by country</span></a></li> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Criticism"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Criticism</span></a></li> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li> 
       </ul> 
      </div> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction_and_methodology">Introduction and methodology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Progress_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Introduction and methodology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The index combines three dimensions </p> 
      <ol>
       <li><a href="/wiki/Maslow%27s_hierarchy_of_needs" title="Maslow's hierarchy of needs">Basic human needs</a></li> 
       <li>Foundations of <a href="/wiki/Well-being" title="Well-being">well-being</a></li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Business_opportunity" title="Business opportunity">Opportunity</a></li>
      </ol> 
      <p>Each dimension includes four components, which are each composed of between three and five specific outcome indicators. The included indicators are selected because they are measured appropriately, with a consistent methodology, by the same organization across all (or essentially all) of the countries in the sample. Together, this framework aims to capture a broad range of interrelated factors revealed by the scholarly literature and practitioner experience as underpinning social progress. </p>
      <p>Two key features of the <i>Social Progress Index</i> are:<sup id="cite_ref-own-website_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-own-website-2">[2]</a></sup> </p> 
      <ol>
       <li>the exclusion of economic variables</li> 
       <li>the use of outcome measures rather than inputs</li>
      </ol> 
      <p>Social Progress Imperative evaluated hundreds of possible indicators while developing the <i>Social Progress Index</i>, including engaging researchers at MIT to determine what indicators best differentiated the performance of nations. The index uses outcome measures when there are sufficient data available or the closest possible proxies.<sup id="cite_ref-own-website_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-own-website-2">[2]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Progress_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>In 2010, a group of scholars and business leaders sought to develop a better measure of a country's level of development and, by extension, better understand its development priorities.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Under the technical guidance of Professors <a href="/wiki/Michael_Porter" title="Michael Porter">Michael Porter</a> from <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Business_School" title="Harvard Business School">Harvard Business School</a> and Scott Stern from the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a>, the group formed a US-based nonprofit called the <a href="/wiki/Social_Progress_Imperative" title="Social Progress Imperative">Social Progress Imperative</a><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and launched a beta version of the <i>Social Progress Index</i> for 50 countries in 2013 to measure a comprehensive array of components of social and environmental performance and aggregate them into an overall framework. </p>
      <p>This work was influenced by the contributions of <a href="/wiki/Amartya_Sen" title="Amartya Sen">Amartya Sen</a> on social development, as well as by the recent call for action in the report <i>Mismeasuring Our Lives</i> by the <a href="/wiki/Commission_on_the_Measurement_of_Economic_Performance_and_Social_Progress" title="Commission on the Measurement of Economic Performance and Social Progress">Commission on the Measurement of Economic Performance and Social Progress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The <i>Social Progress Index</i> was released in 2014 for 133 countries with a second version in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-own-website_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-own-website-2">[2]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 11 July 2013, Social Progress Imperative's chairman and professor at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Business_School" title="Harvard Business School">Harvard Business School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Porter" title="Michael Porter">Michael Porter</a>, addressed the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> 6th Ministerial Forum for Development and discussed the Social Progress Index.<sup id="cite_ref-own-website_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-own-website-2">[2]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In addition to the global <i>Social Progress Index</i>, the methodology used to create it has been adapted to measure social and environmental performance in smaller areas, such as the Amazon region of Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Other projects include a <i>Social Progress Index</i> for the Municipality of Guatemala City.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Fundacion Paraguaya has integrated elements of the <i>Social Progress Index</i> into its <a href="/w/index.php?title=Poverty_Stoplight&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Poverty Stoplight (page does not exist)">Poverty Stoplight</a> tool. The national government of Paraguay is setting a target for <i>Social Progress Index</i> performance alongside GDP targets. </p>
      <p><i>The Guardian</i> reported that the European Commission had agreed to partner with Social Progress Imperative to create a <a href="/wiki/EU_Social_Progress_Index" title="EU Social Progress Index">social progress index for the European Union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The EU Social Progress Index was published in October, 2016. </p>
      <p>A similar index, although with some differences compared to the nation list (and therefore not directly comparable), has been published for the individual <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. states</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="2020_Rankings_and_scores_by_country">2020 Rankings and scores by country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Progress_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 2020 Rankings and scores by country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_Progress_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The index's measure of good governance has been criticised for using data biased against the <a href="/wiki/Global_South" title="Global South">Global South</a>, and some critics [who?] have noted that much of the criteria are based on Western values. There has also been debate on the relevance or accuracy of many of the measurements for gender equality.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> A 2016 survey of online users browsing the SPI website indicated that as one of the index's flaws, 34% of respondents found the data incomplete and/or inaccurate, primarily referencing environmental hazards, energy usage, specific health issues, employment availability and quality, income inequality, gender inequality, and corruption as areas not sufficiently taken into account.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>From an econometric stand point, the Index appears to be similar to other efforts aimed at overcoming the limitation of traditional economic measures such as the <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP). A notable criticism is that although the Social Progress Index can be seen as a superset of indicators used by earlier econometric models such as <a href="/wiki/Gross_National_Well-being" title="Gross National Well-being">Gross National Well-being</a> Index 2005, Bhutan <a href="/wiki/Gross_National_Happiness" title="Gross National Happiness">Gross National Happiness</a> Index of 2012, and <a href="/wiki/World_Happiness_Report" title="World Happiness Report">World Happiness Report</a> of 2012, unlike them, it ignores measures of subjective life satisfaction and psychological well-being. Other critics point out that "there remain certain dimensions that are currently not included in the SPI. These are the concentration of wealth in the top 1 percent of the population, efficiency of the judicial system, and quality of the transportation infrastructure."<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Some critics argue for caution. Though words such as “inclusive capitalism” are now bandied around increasingly to signal a new age, free from ideological battlegrounds between public and private, much of what the organization’s founders say about it confirms that the index is about more “business inclusive” than “inclusive capitalism.”<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> </p>
